# IMPORTANT: Source Code Access

**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

## Your Task

Perfect! I've created a comprehensive analysis of edition-specific import path handling in rust-lang/rust. Here's a summary of my findings:

## Key Findings

I've identified **17 critical source files** implementing edition-specific import path handling, organized into three categories:

### Compiler Implementation (6 files)
- **`standard_library_imports.rs`**: Central coordinator for edition-based prelude injection with `#[macro_use]`
- **`resolver/lib.rs`**: Core types `ExternPrelude` and `ModuleOrUniformRoot` for edition-aware path resolution
- **`resolver/ident.rs`**: Path resolution logic that differs by edition (PathRoot handling)
- **`resolver/imports.rs`**: Import resolution with edition-specific semantics
- **`resolver/build_reduced_graph.rs`**: Macro use import processing
- **`resolver/diagnostics.rs`**: Edition-aware error suggestions

### Standard Library (6 files)
- **`std/lib.rs`** and **`core/lib.rs`**: Use `#[prelude_import]` attribute for edition-based prelude selection
- **`std/prelude/mod.rs`** and **`core/prelude/mod.rs`**: Define edition-specific modules (v1, rust_2015, rust_2018, rust_2021, rust_2024, rust_future)
- **`std/prelude/v1.rs`** and **`core/prelude/v1.rs`**: Base prelude implementations

### Analysis & Testing (5 files)
- **`hir-def/nameres/collector.rs`**: Edition-aware name resolution in Rust Analyzer
- **`tests/ui/rust-2018/uniform-paths/`**: Comprehensive test suite validating edition 2018 uniform paths

## Major Mechanisms

1. **Path Starting Semantics**: Paths starting with `::` resolve differently—2015 edition uses module-and-extern-prelude fallback, while 2018+ uses direct extern prelude
2. **Prelude Differences**: Each edition gets progressively more exports (2021 adds FromIterator/TryFrom/TryInto; 2024 adds Future/IntoFuture)

Preludes can be seen as a pattern to make using multiple types more\\n16: //! convenient. As such, you'll find other preludes in the standard library,\\n17: //! such as [`std::io::prelude`]. Various libraries in the Rust ecosystem may\\n18: //! also define their own preludes.\\n19: //!\\n20: //! [`std::io::prelude`]: crate::io::prelude\\n21: //!\\n22: //! The difference between 'the prelude' and these other preludes is that they\\n23: //! are not automatically `use`'d, and must be imported manually. This is still\\n24: //! easier than importing all of their constituent components.\\n25: //!\\n26: //! # Prelude contents\\n27: //!\\n28: //! This type is so\\n62: //!   commonly used, its variants are also exported.\\n63: //! * <code>[std::result]::[Result]::{[self][Result], [Ok], [Err]}</code>, a type\\n64: //!   for functions that may succeed or fail.
